    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="acb335dbbbe047d38547703a9fffd9d6">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'eb8f1a8a-3090-432f-b321-7d5670513641'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d4633428" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered sodium-ion oxides hold considerable promise in achieving high-performance sodium-ion batteries. However, the notorious phase transformation during charging, attributed to increased O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion, results in substantial performance decay. Here, a hierarchical layer modification strategy is proposed to stabilize interlayer repulsion. During desodiation, migrated Li<sup>+</sup> from the transition metal layer and anchored Ca<sup>2+</sup> in sodium sites maintain the cationic content within the sodium layer. Meanwhile, partial oxygen substitution by fluorine and the involvement of oxygen in redox reactions increase the average valence of the oxygen layer. This sustained cation presence and elevated anion valence collectively mitigate increasing O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion during sodium extraction, enabling the Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (NCLNMOF) cathode to retain a pure P2-type structure across a wide voltage range. Unexpected insights reveal the interplay between different doping elements: the robust Li─F bonds and Ca<sup>2+</sup> steric effects suppressing Li<sup>+</sup> loss. The NCLNMOF electrode exhibits 82.5% capacity retention after 1000 cycles and a high-rate capability of 94 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating the efficacy of hierarchical layer modification for high-performance layered oxide cathodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407519-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407519-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The continuous advancement of secondary batteries has greatly facilitated the mobile lifestyle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0001_R_d4633268e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the secondary battery market for several decades, the considerable rise in prices of lithium salts (e.g., Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) in recent years has motivated researchers to explore more cost-effective energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0002_R_d4633268e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Joule. Acc. Chem. Res. Nat. Energy.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Given the physical and chemical similarities between sodium and lithium, the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as a complementary technology to LIBs has emerged as a straightforward and promising research direction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0003_R_d4633268e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev. Nat. Rev. Mater. Science. Interdiscip. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The intrinsic nature of sodium, including the larger atomic mass and higher standard electrode potential, renders SIBs less competitive than LIBs in terms of specific energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0004_R_d4633268e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl. Nat. Chem.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the anode materials, hard carbon materials can now reach up to 400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a reversible low-voltage plateau (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V) in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0005_R_d4633268e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the sodium-based cathode materials, in terms of specific energy density and cycle life, are far below their lithium counterparts,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0006_R_d4633268e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - InfoMat.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> such as commercialized layered LiCoO<sub>2</sub> and nickel-rich materials. Having a similar layered structure, P2-type Na─Ni─Mn─O systems based on Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox, are particularly favorable among various cathode materials due to their high discharge plateau and large specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0007_R_d4633268e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun. Nano Energy. Carbon Neutralization. eScience.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, similar to the notorious phase transformation in layered LiCoO<sub>2</sub> and nickel-rich materials at high states of charge (SoC),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0008_R_d4633268e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Energy Chem. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the detrimental P2–O2 phase transformation leads to the rapid capacity decay of P2-type Na─Ni─Mn─O electrodes during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0009_R_d4633268e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacity retention of a P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) electrode cycled between 2 and 4&nbsp;V for 100 cycles is 92%, whereas this retention plunges to 29% when the upper cutoff voltage is increased to 4.5 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0010_R_d4633268e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Electrochim. Acta.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The root cause of the detrimental P2–O2 phase transformation is the electrostatic repulsion interaction between O<sup>2−</sup> ions in the Na layers. At high SoC, the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> gradually results in the presence of face-to-face O<sup>2−</sup> ions between adjacent transition metal (TM) layers. The escalating O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> electrostatic repulsion would lead to the expansion of the Na layer spacing. Consequently, the oversized Na layers fail to sustain the P2-type structure, causing the transition to a thermodynamically stable O2-type stacking as the TM layers glide randomly between (1/3, 2/3, <i>z</i>) and (2/3, 1/3, <i>z</i>) directions for the opposite relative glide.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0011_R_d4633268e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Chem. Soc. Rev.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The appearance of the O2 phase structure is accompanied by a rapid contraction of the <i>c</i>-parameter and lattice volume. This phenomenon hampers Na<sup>+</sup> transportation, triggers particle fragmentation, and fosters TM layer stacking faults.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0012_R_d4633268e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy. Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In previous reports, diverse heterovalent ions have been introduced into the Na layer (K<sup>+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, and Mg<sup>2+</sup>, etc.),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0013_R_d4633268e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> TM layer (Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, etc.),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0012_R_d4633268e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy. Adv. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0014_R_d4633268e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater. J. Power Sources. Angew. Chem. J. Mater. Chem. A. Nano Energy.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and O layer (F<sup>−</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0015_R_d4633268e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> of materials to realize the single-layer modification and mitigate the P2–O2 phase transformation. However, due to the O<sup>2−</sup>–O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion being simultaneously affected by Na layer, TM layer, and O layer, the efficacy of single-layer modification is limited.</p>
         
         <p>This work provides a rational hierarchical layer modification strategy aimed at stabilizing interlayer electrostatic repulsion and preserving Na layer spacing in layered metal oxide materials. Taking P2-type NNMO as a model material, we strategically incorporate Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, and F<sup>−</sup> elements into the Na layer, TM layer, and O layer, respectively, of layered metal oxides. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) results confirm that the P2-Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (NCLNMOF) material maintains the P2 phase structure within a wide voltage range with a negligible volume change of 3.1%, far less than that of the pristine NNMO material (27.6%). Besides, solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (SS NMR) and density functional theory (DFT) calculation results further reveal that the robust Li─F bonds and the steric effect of Ca<sup>2+</sup> can prevent electrode failure by suppressing the Li<sup>+</sup> loss from the bulk material. Consequently, the NCLNMOF electrode exhibits a robust long-range crystal framework and a reversible ionic local environment, achieving excellent long-term cyclability and remarkable rate capability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407519-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407519-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407519-sec-0030-title">2.1 Hierarchical Layer Modification Strategy</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The substantial decline in the high-voltage plateau is the root cause of the notable deterioration in discharge capacity, with the P2–O2 phase transformation adversely affecting Na<sup>+</sup> transport and cycle performance in the NNMO electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Tuning the electrostatic repulsion interaction between O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> in the Na layers is regarded as an effective way of suppressing the notorious P2–O2 phase transformation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0016_R_d4633268e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1a</a>, two main approaches have been proposed here for reducing O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion.

               <ul class="rlist hanging">
                  
                  <li><span class="number">I. </span>Increasing the average oxidation state of anions in O layer, which involves:</li>
                  
                  <li><span class="number">i) </span>Replacing O2− with monovalent anions such as F− in the O layers.</li>
                  
                  <li><span class="number">ii) </span>Triggering the anionic redox reaction to form On− (n &lt; 2) at high SoC.</li>
                  
                  <li><span class="number">II. </span>Maintaining some “pillar” cations in the Na layers at high SoC to shield the O2−─O2− repulsion:</li>
                  
                  <li><span class="number">iii) </span>Introducing the pillar ions into the Na layers.</li>
                  
                  <li><span class="number">iv) </span>Doping the reversible migrating cations into the TM layers.</li>
                  </ul>
            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407519-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ad9115e-ed0d-43fd-a7b3-5822fa500785/adma202407519-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ad9115e-ed0d-43fd-a7b3-5822fa500785/adma202407519-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/62502a54-5456-43a2-bc95-574180c920c5/adma202407519-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ad9115e-ed0d-43fd-a7b3-5822fa500785/adma202407519-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407519-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Crystal structure and morphology characterizations of synthesized powder materials. a) Schematic illustration of reduced interlayer repulsion in layered oxide materials. b) SEM images of Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (NCLNMOF) and c) EDS mapping results showing the distribution of Ca, F, Mn, and Ni elements. d) Powder XRD patterns of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO), Na<sub>0.57</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NCNMO), Na<sub>0.76</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NLNMO), Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (NNMOF), and NCLNMOF materials. e) Refined XRD pattern of NCLNMOF material. f) The Na layer spacing of NNMO, NCNMO, NLNMO, NNMOF, and NCLNMOF materials obtained from XRD refinement. g) HRTEM image of NCLNMOF material with annotation of the (002) spacing and the distance between adjacent TM ions. h) Schematic illustration of the effects of hierarchical layer modification on the material structure.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on the above analysis, we selectively doped Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, and F<sup>−</sup> ions into the Na layer, TM layer, and O layer of NNMO material, respectively, following the principle of similar ionic radii (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). By referring to the optimal doping amounts of each element reported in previous literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0013_R_d4633268e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#adma202407519-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0015_R_d4633268e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and conducting electrochemical tests and structure characterization on a series of materials (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), we synthesized an optimized NCLNMOF material by a simple one-step solid-state method, and the single-element-doped materials, including Na<sub>0.57</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NCNMO), Na<sub>0.76</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NLNMO), and Na<sub>0.61</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (NNMOF) materials, were also prepared for the comparative analysis. Each material conforms to the designed stoichiometry (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1b</a>, the synthesized NCLNMOF material exhibits irregular flake-like morphology with particle sizes ranging from 1.2 to 5.4&nbsp;µm. The uniform distribution of Na, Ca, F, Ni, Mn, and O elements in the bulk of particles is confirmed by energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information)). The crystal structure of samples was revealed through powder XRD in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1d</a>. All materials exhibit a pure P2 phase structure with space group <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i>, indicating successful incorporation of the selectively doped ions into NNMO without generating impurity phases. The refined XRD patterns using general structure analysis system (GSAS)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0017_R_d4633268e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are further shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The refinement results confirm the distribution of Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, and F<sup>−</sup> ions in the Na layer, TM layer, and O layer of materials, respectively. Due to the higher valence state of Ca<sup>2+</sup> compared to Na<sup>+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup> tends to occupy the Na edge sites in the Na layer to alleviate electrostatic repulsion from neighboring TM ions, which is consistent with our subsequent theoretical calculation results.<sup>19</sup>F SS NMR results confirm that F<sup>−</sup> has been successfully incorporated into the O layer (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the size of the layer spacing could reflect the magnitude of the O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> electrostatic forces in Na layers within a similar material system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0015_R_d4633268e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0018_R_d4633268e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1f</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), all doped materials exhibit smaller interlayer spacing compared to NNMO, especially for the NCLNMOF displaying the smallest interlayer spacing of Na layers (3.640 Å), corresponding to the lowest electrostatic repulsion between O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> in the Na layers of NCLNMOF. The calculated ratio of the Na layer spacing (<i>d</i><sub>O─Na─O</sub>) to the TM layer spacing (<i>d</i><sub>O─TM─O</sub>) is 1.92 for NCLNMOF material, which leads to the desired P2-type structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0003_R_d4633268e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev. Nat. Rev. Mater. Science. Interdiscip. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0018_R_d4633268e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was employed to gain further insights into the crystal structure of the NCLNMOF material. The bright spots correspond to the TM columns, while the dark spots represent the Na columns, indicating a well-organized layered structure along the [010] zone axis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1g</a>). Notably, the measurement of the (002) interlayer spacing in the NCLNMOF material yielded a value of <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> = 5.53 Å, consistent with the refined XRD results (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The hierarchical layer modification additionally facilitates the formation of Na─F (477.3&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) and Ca─F (529&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) bonds in the NCLNMOF material, which exhibit stronger bond energies compared to Ni─F (439.7 ± 5.9&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) and Mn─F (445.2 ± 7.5&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0019_R_d4633268e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These structural modifications collectively contribute to the reduction in the Na layer spacing, Na/Ca─F bond length, and TM─O/F─TM angle (<i>θ</i>). Conversely, the TM─O/F bond length increases, along with the broadening of the TM layer, as schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0001">1h</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407519-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical characteristics of the NCLNMOF electrode were comprehensively evaluated in both half-cell and full-cell configurations. The first charge–discharge curves of prepared materials are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2a</a>. The initial Coulombic efficiency of all prepared cathodes exceeds 100% corresponding to the sodium deficiency characteristic of P2-type materials (Na ≈ 0.67). Among them, the NNMOF electrode exhibits the highest initial discharge capacity of 215 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which should be attributed to the enhanced capacity reversibility above 4.2&nbsp;V owing to F-doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0015_R_d4633268e976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0020_R_d4633268e976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as evidenced by the higher reversible plateau capacity with smaller voltage hysteresis of NNMOF at 4.2&nbsp;V compared to that of NNMO (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The sustained presence of O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup> ions (530.7&nbsp;eV)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0021_R_d4633268e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in the X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) O 1s spectra of the NNMOF electrode after cycling suggests that F-doping may improve the reversibility of oxygen reactions (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), which is likely due to F<sup>−</sup> enhances the flexibility of Mn─O covalent bonding<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0020_R_d4633268e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and suppresses oxygen evolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0020_R_d4633268e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the initial discharge capacities of the other electrodes are lower than that of pristine NNMO, such as 180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for NCNMO and the lowest capacity of 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for NLNMO. The NCLNMOF electrode demonstrates an acceptable discharge capacity of 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The reduced capacity of NCLNMOF compared to NNMO should be attributed to Ca<sup>2+</sup> partially occupying Na<sup>+</sup> sites and Li<sup>+</sup> in the TM layer, reducing the content of Ni ions as the redox centers. The discharge capacity ratio of electrodes in different voltage ranges is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), with the NCLNMOF electrode exhibiting the highest capacity ratio in the optimal medium voltage range (3.0–4.0&nbsp;V). A comparison of the charge–discharge and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2a,b</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information)) indicates a smoother charge–discharge curve for the NCLNMOF electrode, resembling solid-solution reactions. This smoother curve should ascribe to the effective suppression of plateaus corresponding to Na-vacancy ordering (≈3.25 and ≈3.65&nbsp;V) and P2–O2 phase transition (≈4.2&nbsp;V), which will be further discussed below. As a result, the cliff-like drop in <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at 4.2&nbsp;V is greatly suppressed in NCLNMOF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2c</a>). This confirms the elimination of Na<sup>+</sup> transportation hindrance caused by the P2–O2 phase transition and leads to the geometric mean value of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> in NCLNMOF (1.249 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), which is 22.5 times higher than that in NNMO electrode (5.548 × 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). The enhancement of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is expected to improve the rate capability. Thus, the NCLNMOF electrode displays high capacity and stable cycling at each current density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2d</a>). At 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the initial capacity of NCLNMOF is 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the corresponding capacity retention when the current density increased by tenfold, 30-fold, 50-fold, and 100-fold are 90.7%, 74.8%, 66.9%, and 53.4%, respectively, demonstrating the exceptional rate capability of the NCLNMOF electrode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407519-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a6aac26-1118-4547-a397-298f28ae6af9/adma202407519-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a6aac26-1118-4547-a397-298f28ae6af9/adma202407519-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0bea49a3-2af6-44df-a408-048062d1b62f/adma202407519-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a6aac26-1118-4547-a397-298f28ae6af9/adma202407519-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407519-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance characterization of synthesized cathodes in half-cell and full-cell configurations. a) First charge–discharge curves of the prepared materials at 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Comparison of d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of NNMO and NCLNMOF electrodes during the 1st cycle. c) Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique profile and calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of the NCLNMOF electrode during the 2nd cycle. d) Rate performance characterization of the prepared materials at current densities ranging from 16 to 1600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Discharge capacity and f) average discharge voltage evolution of NNMO, NCNMO, NLNMO, NNMOF, and NCLNMOF electrodes at 160&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Cycling performance of NCLNMOF electrode at 1600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. h) Comparison of cycling performance of the NCLNMOF and NNMO electrodes at 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. i) Capacity comparison between NCLNMOF cathode and hard carbon anode at 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. j) Long-term cycling performance test of the NCLNMOF || hard carbon full cell at 80&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, with the schematic of the full cell shown in the inset figure.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To comprehensively assess the electrochemical performance of the prepared electrodes, the long-term cycling performance was evaluated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2e,f</a>). The electrodes were activated for two cycles at a current density of 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, followed by a prolonged cycling test at 160&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. After 200 cycles, the discharge capacities of NNMO, NCNMO, NLNMO, NNMOF, and NCLNMOF electrodes are 14, 78, 88, 29, and 103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Remarkably, the NCLNMOF electrode demonstrates superior cyclability, delivering a discharge capacity of 101 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after 300 cycles, with an outstanding capacity retention of 82.2%. Of note, the average discharge voltage is also a crucial parameter and mutually determines the energy density of batteries with discharge capacity. For instance, cathode materials involving anionic redox reaction would possess high initial capacity and acceptable capacity retention, but fast voltage decay is unfavorable for preserving the energy density of battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0022_R_d4633268e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2f</a> shows that the evolution of the average discharge voltage varies with the cycle number for different electrodes. The NCLNMOF electrode exhibits an initial discharge average voltage of ≈3.2&nbsp;V at 160&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and a notably high voltage retention of 98.5% after 200 cycles, whereas that number only 75.1% in NNMO. Interestingly, both NNMO and NNMOF electrodes display a brief increase in capacity and voltage following an initial rapid decline during early cycling. This phenomenon, also observed in related reports, tends to vanish with metal ion doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0023_R_d4633268e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A. Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We tentatively attribute this phenomenon to the impact of the P2–O2 phase transition on bulk structure and ion transport, meriting further detailed investigation in future studies. Moreover, when subjected to a long-term cycling test at a high current density of 1600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2g</a>), NCLNMOF electrode delivers a discharge capacity of 94 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after the activated process and the capacity retention remains notably high at 94.7% after 500 cycles and 82.5% after 1000 cycles (calculated based on the maximum capacity during the cycling). On the other hand, conducting cycling performance tests at small current densities provides a better evaluation of the electrode cyclability, as it alleviates the impact of electrochemical polarization and allows for more Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation and more obvious structural changes. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2h</a>, the NCLNMOF electrode demonstrates a capacity retention of 89.5% after 100 cycles (operating time exceeding 1700 h) at 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, while NNMO shows a poor capacity retention of 41.2% after 50 cycles. To validate the practicality of the electrode, the full-cell configuration was assembled by matching NCLNMOF cathode with hard carbon anode (reversible capacity of 320 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at a weight ratio of 2.1:1 to balance the capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2i</a>). The electrode presodiation procedure and the effects are detailed in the Experimental Section and illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0002">2j</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), within the voltage of 1.4–4.2&nbsp;V, the full cell delivers an initial discharge capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> with the operation voltage of 3.31&nbsp;V, corresponding to a promising energy density of ≈273&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the active material mass of both electrodes). Furthermore, the full cell exhibits satisfactory cycling stability with a capacity retention of 92.4% after 100 cycles at 80&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) provides a comparative analysis of the electrochemical performance of NCLNMOF with recent studies on NNMO modification. In short, these electrochemical results demonstrate that the hierarchical layer modification imparts superior rate capability and energy density stability to the NCLNMOF electrode, even without any interfacial or electrolyte modification.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407519-sec-0050-title">2.3 The Long-Range Structural Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>Long-range structural evolution of NNMO and NCLNMOF electrodes were monitored by in situ XRD measurements (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0003">3</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information)). Significant structural change happens when the voltage exceeds 4.2&nbsp;V. The (002) peak of NNMO electrode gradually disappears and is replaced by a new peak appearing at 2<i>θ</i> = 20°, signaling the typical P2–O2 phase transformation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0003">3a</a>). Eventually, the O2 phase (space group: <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>mc</i>) dominates the XRD pattern of NNMO electrode at 4.3&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Though the P2 phase can be reversed back after discharging, the large voltage hysteresis observed between the initial generation (charge to 4.2&nbsp;V) and disappearance (discharge to 3.7&nbsp;V) of O2 phase confirms the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transportation during the biphasic phase transformation region. Different from the biphasic phase transformation in NNMO electrode, NCLNMOF electrode preserves the P2 structure well and exhibits a reversible solid-state reaction behavior during the charging–discharging processes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information)). In particular, upon deep desodiation, the (00<i>l</i>) diffraction peaks smoothly shift toward higher diffraction angles, which is attributed to the involvement of O<sup>2−</sup> in charge compensation and Li<sup>+</sup> migration from the TM layers into adjacent Na layers, thereby reducing O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion and leading to a slight decrease in interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0014_R_d4633268e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater. J. Power Sources. Angew. Chem. J. Mater. Chem. A. Nano Energy.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0024_R_d4633268e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Joule.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the XRD refinements were conducted to obtain the refined lattice parameters during cycling. Notably, within 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V, the <i>c</i>-parameter and lattice volume of NNMO undergo changes of 22.5% and 27.6% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0003">3b,c</a>), respectively, which is considerably larger than the <i>c</i>-parameter (2.8%) and lattice volume (3.1%) changes of NCLNMOF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0003">3e,f</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information)). Furthermore, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information), NNMO experiences repeated phase transformation and substantial lattice parameter changes during cycling, leading to obvious TM layer distortion and stress accumulation. By contrast, NCLNMOF maintains single P2 phase structure throughout the charge–discharge processes, thus preserving its crystal structure even after long-term cycling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407519-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/57eb0a49-72d0-4a7a-bb59-2a0dbe182c84/adma202407519-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/57eb0a49-72d0-4a7a-bb59-2a0dbe182c84/adma202407519-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/45ff156d-9b49-485a-960c-40f067b4cf0a/adma202407519-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/57eb0a49-72d0-4a7a-bb59-2a0dbe182c84/adma202407519-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407519-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD characterization of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> cathodes. The evolution of b) the <i>c</i>-parameter and c) lattice volume of NNMO electrode. In situ XRD contour maps of d) NCLNMOF electrode during the initial cycles within the voltage window of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V at a low current of 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.
