The corresponding charge–discharge curves labeled on the left side of each in situ XRD contour map and the evolution of the lattice parameters are obtained through refined XRD analysis.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407519-sec-0060-title">2.4 The Local Environment Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The ex situ SS NMR technique was further employed to investigate the local environment evolution of <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>7</sup>Li in the electrodes during electrochemical reactions. The <sup>23</sup>Na magic angle spin (MAS) NMR spectra of NMMO and NCLNMOF electrodes are compared in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0004">4a,b</a>. Due to the stronger Fermi-contact interaction between TM ions and Na<sup>+</sup> through the TM─O─Na pathway, the pristine NCLNMOF (1623&nbsp;ppm) electrode exhibits a higher chemical shift compared to that of NNMO (1479&nbsp;ppm), which is consistent with the smaller Na layer spacing and shorter TM─O─Na bond length in NCLNMOF. During charging process, the oxidation of Ni ions is accompanied by their decreased paramagnetic contribution to Na<sup>+</sup>. Consequently, the <sup>23</sup>Na signals of both NNMO and NCLNMOF electrodes move toward lower chemical shifts. The lower intensity and chemical shift of NNMO electrode correspond to its deeper desodiation process at 4.3&nbsp;V, which is in agreement with its higher initial charge capacity. However, the <sup>23</sup>Na signal of NNMO is significantly broadened and difficult to resolve at 1.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0004">4a</a>), which could be attributed to the reduction of Mn ions and the accumulation of structural defects after the biphasic phase transformation in NNMO. Notably, the <sup>23</sup>Na signal of NCLNMOF is highly reversible upon discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V, demonstrating the preservation of the short-range structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0004">4b</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407519-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4e8a6566-626b-4064-a7e6-6b07e33a406a/adma202407519-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4e8a6566-626b-4064-a7e6-6b07e33a406a/adma202407519-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4a1bb1e0-df28-49af-a282-a43824887f09/adma202407519-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4e8a6566-626b-4064-a7e6-6b07e33a406a/adma202407519-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407519-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>7</sup>Li MAS SS NMR characterization of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.76</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> cathodes. <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra during the initial charge–discharge cycles of a) NNMO and b) NCLNMOF electrodes at pristine, charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, and discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V states. The asterisk (*) indicates isotropic signals. Integration results of different Li signals in c) NLNMO and d) NCLNMOF electrodes at different SoCs. <sup>7</sup>Li stepwise spin-echo NMR spectra of e) NLNMO and f) NCLNMOF electrodes at different SoCs, with the corresponding charge–discharge curves labeled on the left side.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Noted that the irreversible loss of Li<sup>+</sup> during cycling in Li-doped NNMO is the culprit of performance degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0025_R_d4633268e1418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Li<sup>+</sup> preservation and local environment in NLNMO and NCLNMOF electrodes was further characterized by <sup>7</sup>Li MAS NMR at different SoCs. The combination of projection of magic-angle turning phase-adjusted spinning sidebands (pj-MATPASS) and stepwise spin-echo sequence guarantee the quantitatively analysis of different Li<sup>+</sup> sites (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The isotropic signals obtained by pj-MATPASS are used to perform peak fitting of the spin-echo spectra. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0004">4e,f</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), <sup>7</sup>Li spectra contain three regions: i) chemical shifts greater than 1100&nbsp;ppm relate to the Li<sup>+</sup> located in the TM layers, ii) the signals between 0 and 1100&nbsp;ppm correspond to Li<sup>+</sup> in the alkali metal layer, iii) 0&nbsp;ppm signal corresponds to the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) species: such as LiF, Li<sub>2</sub>O, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0014_R_d4633268e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater. J. Power Sources. Angew. Chem. J. Mater. Chem. A. Nano Energy.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0026_R_d4633268e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The detailed assignment of <sup>7</sup>Li NMR signals is provided in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). When charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, the Li<sup>+</sup> content in the TM layer decreases significantly while the Li<sup>+</sup> content in the Na layer increases for NLNMO electrode, which is known as Li<sup>+</sup>-migration mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0026_R_d4633268e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">26</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0027_R_d4633268e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, the peak shape and intensity of <sup>7</sup>Li in NCLNMOF is almost maintained. The integrated <sup>7</sup>Li signal shows quantitative results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0004">4c,d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information)). Nearly 39.9% Li<sup>+</sup> in NLNMO migrates from TM layer to Na layer. This extensive migration would result in the creation of more ion vacancies in the TM layer, which promotes the interlayer migration of TM ions and ultimately causes degradation of structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0028_R_d4633268e1490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nature.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This number is only 3.5% in NCLNMOF, demonstrating that the introduction of Ca<sup>2+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> helps to stabilize the Li<sup>+</sup> local environment at high voltages. In addition, 0&nbsp;ppm signal, which is typically ascribed to the CEI Li<sup>+</sup>, also shows remarkable increase after charge in NLNMO. This implies that, despite the Li migration within bulk materials, some Li<sup>+</sup> would “leach out” from bulk materials and lead to the gradual failure of the Li<sup>+</sup>-doping strategy of NLNMO. This loss is greatly minimized in NCLNMOF. Consequently, the Li<sub>TM2</sub> and Li<sub>TM3</sub> signals of NLNMO disappear, and the intensity of <sup>7</sup>Li signals in the Na layer region decreases significantly, while the <sup>7</sup>Li signal of NCLNMOF shows a completely reversible recovery after discharge. This structural reversibility firmly demonstrates that the hierarchical layer modification can effectively suppress the irreversible Li<sup>+</sup> migration, thereby enhancing the stability of the electrode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407519-sec-0070-title">2.5 Ion Migration Mechanisms</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the cation arrangements within the NCLNMOF structure at pristine and charged states, we employed the Ewald summation method to select 100 structures with the lowest electrostatic energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0029_R_d4633268e1529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Comput. Phys. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, generalized gradient approximation (GGA) + <i>U</i> calculations optimized these structures to obtain the most stable configuration. Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) presents the 20 lowest energy structures, revealing a tendency of ionic aggregation for Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, and F<sup>−</sup> ions within the optimized NCLNMOF structures. Specifically, close spatial proximity between Li<sup>+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> ions leads to the formation of a robust Li─F bond,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0030_R_d4633268e1550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions in the Na layer occupy prismatic sites adjacent to the LiO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons to alleviate electrostatic repulsion from the TM ions. The climbing-image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method was employed to calculate energy barriers for Na<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> migration within the Na layer (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0013_R_d4633268e1568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The computational results in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0005">5a</a> show a significantly higher energy barrier of 1.05&nbsp;eV for Ca<sup>2+</sup> migration. This is in stark contrast to the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>, exhibiting a much lower energy barrier of 0.36&nbsp;eV.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407519-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/08ff2187-0851-4f7b-93be-ffe16a7c0e3f/adma202407519-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/08ff2187-0851-4f7b-93be-ffe16a7c0e3f/adma202407519-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7b8b81f6-e50a-4178-a5ab-5d051a537b69/adma202407519-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/08ff2187-0851-4f7b-93be-ffe16a7c0e3f/adma202407519-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407519-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculations of ion migration mechanisms and XAFS characterizations of Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub>. a) The migration energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the Na layer. b) Schematic of the migration pathway of Li<sup>+</sup> from initial to intermediate and final sites. c) Li<sup>+</sup> relative site energies of intermediate and final sites calculated along the paths A. d) Li<sup>+</sup> relative site energies of intermediate sites calculated along the paths B. e) Ni K-edge and f) Mn K-edge XAFS spectra of Na<sub>0.61</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>[Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> electrodes at different states of charge.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Considering the challenge of Ca<sup>2+</sup> extraction, we utilized a simplified model focusing solely on Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the Na layers to calculate the relative site energies governing Li<sup>+</sup> migration between the TM and Na layers at high SoC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0022_R_d4633268e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two potential pathways for Li<sup>+</sup> migration are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0005">5b</a>. Path A represents Li<sup>+</sup> migration into a Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free Na layer, while Path B corresponds to Li<sup>+</sup> migration into a Na layer with Ca<sup>2+</sup>. Along Path A, Li<sup>+</sup> first migrates to the intermediate site below the TM layer before reaching the adjacent final site (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). Comparative analysis of the site energies for Li-doped NLNMO, Li<sup>+</sup>-and-F<sup>−</sup>-codoped NLNMOF, and NCLNMOF materials reveals that NLNMO exhibits the lowest relative site energies, with +0.15&nbsp;eV from pristine to intermediate sites and a negative value of −0.24&nbsp;eV from intermediate to final sites. This suggests that Li<sup>+</sup> migration from TM layer to Na layer occurs at high SoC for NLNMO, and Li<sup>+</sup> migration in Na layers is highly thermodynamically favorable. Similarly, for Path B (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information), when Li migrates to the intermediate site, NLNMO displays the lowest relative site energy of −0.23&nbsp;eV. In stark contrast, when attempting the migration of Li<sup>+</sup> along Path B in NCLNMOF toward the intermediate site within the Na layer, the establishment of a stable structure becomes unattainable. Instead, the Li<sup>+</sup> ions revert back to the TM layer, attributed to substantial electrostatic repulsion from nearby Ca<sup>2+</sup>. This phenomenon reveals the effective suppression of Li<sup>+</sup> interlayer migration. The agreement between the theoretical calculations and above SS NMR results underscores the effective role of the strong Li─F bond and the steric effect of Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions in modulating the interlayer Li<sup>+</sup> migration and mitigating bulk phase Li<sup>+</sup> extraction.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407519-sec-0080-title">2.6 Charge Compensation</h3>
            
            <p>To gain insights into the charge compensation mechanism of NCLNMOF during electrochemical reaction, we performed theoretical calculations Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ca<sub>1</sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>6</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>47</sub>F<sub>1</sub> (<i>x</i> = 3, 15, 18) supercells to obtain the partial density of states results. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information), in the pristine state (<i>x</i> = 15), Ni 3d states predominantly constitute the valence bands near the Fermi level, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> extraction is primarily compensated by Ni ions. As Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted, O 2p states become the major contributors to the top valence bands of Na<sub>3</sub>Ca<sub>1</sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>6</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>47</sub>F<sub>1</sub>, suggesting the involvement of O<sup>2−</sup> in high-voltage redox. At the end of discharge (<i>x</i> = 18), the valence bands near the Fermi level are dominated by Mn 3d states. To further investigate charge compensation mechanisms, X-ray absorption fine structure (XAFS) spectra for Ni K-edge and Mn K-edge of NCLNMOF electrodes were collected at different SoCs during the first cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0005">5e,f</a>). During the charging process, Mn K-edge spectra exhibit negligible changes, corresponding to the bulk Mn ions maintaining the Mn<sup>4+</sup> state. While the Ni K-edge continuously shifts to higher energy, predominantly between the pristine and 4.2&nbsp;V states. At 4.3 and 4.4&nbsp;V, the Ni K-edge spectra closely resemble that of the Ni K-edge spectrum at the 4.2&nbsp;V, implying that lattice oxygen redox reaction governs charge compensation above 4.2&nbsp;V. This aligns with XPS results, revealing evident O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup> ions (<i>n</i> &lt; 2) originating from oxidized lattice oxygen atoms when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). The occurrence of oxygen redox reaction may be attributed to the Li─O─Li, Li─O─Na, and □─O─Na structural configuration in NCLNMOF material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0031_R_d4633268e1776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Storage Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon discharging to 3&nbsp;V, the Mn K-edge remains unchanged, while the Ni K-edge returns to its initial state, affirming the reversible recovery of Ni ions to the Ni<sup>2+</sup> state. Further discharge to 1.5&nbsp;V shows no notable change in the Ni K-edge, while the Mn K-edge shifts to lower energy, illustrating that Mn ions govern charge compensation when discharged below 3&nbsp;V. The combined theoretical calculations and experimental results collectively demonstrate the participation of Mn, Ni, and O ions in the charge compensation reactions during cycling, with anionic redox reaction occurring at the end of charge to form O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup> (<i>n</i> &lt; 2) ions. The emergence of O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup> ions has been previously confirmed to effectively reduce the interlayer repulsion during desodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0024_R_d4633268e1795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Joule.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the average repulsion between the configurations such as F<sup>−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup>, F<sup>−</sup>─F<sup>−</sup>, F<sup>−</sup>─O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup>, O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup>─O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup>, and O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> in NCLNMOF at high voltage are believed to be lower compared to O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> configuration in pristine materials. Moreover, operando differential electrochemical mass spectrometer (DEMS) tests confirm the absence of O<sub>2</sub> evolution during the voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information), proving the high reversibility of lattice oxygen redox reaction in NCLNMOF electrode. Although anionic redox reactions have also been confirmed in the original NNMO material,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0007_R_d4633268e1840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun. Nano Energy. Carbon Neutralization. eScience.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202407519-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0032_R_d4633268e1840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Energy.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it fails to prevent the P2–O2 phase transformation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407519-fig-0003">3a</a>), which further underscores the efficacy of the hierarchical layer modification strategy in facilitating synergistic interactions among the Na layer, TM layer, and O layer.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407519-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407519-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the above results clearly demonstrate the effective suppression of relative TM layer gliding and the P2–O2 phase transition in NNMO cathodes through hierarchical layer modification to stabilize interlayer O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> electrostatic repulsion. The presence of anchored Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the Na layers and Li<sup>+</sup> migration from the TM layers works synergistically to maintain cation content and thus shields the O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion in the Na layers during charging. Additionally, the substitution of F<sup>−</sup> in the O layers and the involvement of oxygen in redox reactions increase the oxidation state of O<sup>2−</sup> at high SoC. These combined mechanisms collectively reduce the O<sup>2−</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> repulsion and lattice volume changes of the optimal NCLNMOF electrode during cycling. In particular, the effectiveness of the hierarchical layer modification strategy in suppressing the P2–O2 phase transition is evidenced by in situ XRD results, leading to the solid-solution behavior of NCLNMOF electrode across the whole voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. SS NMR techniques combined with theoretical calculations reveal another benefit arising from the hierarchical layer modification. The strategic introduction of Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, and F<sup>−</sup> at specific lattice sites induces a synergistic effect, leading to beneficial ion aggregation and cooperative interactions: the incorporation of F<sup>−</sup> within the O layer gives rise to the formation of strong Li─F bonds, while Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the Na layers shows a significant steric effect, effectively preventing Li<sup>+</sup> loss from the bulk. In virtue of stable long-range crystal structure and local environment, NCLNMOF electrode exhibits high-rate capability at room temperature and stable long-term cycling performance over 1000 cycles. This work presents how “stabilizing interlayer repulsion” can serve as a guiding principle for the design of hierarchical layer modification, and offers a novel pathway for synthesizing layered metal oxide cathodes that exhibit low volume strain, exceptional structural stability, and prolonged cycling lifespan.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407519-sec-0100">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407519-sec-0100-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0110">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0120">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202407519-sec-0130-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The pristine NNMO, single-element-doped NCNMO, NLNMO, NNMOF, and multielement-doped NCLNMOF materials were synthesized via the high-temperature solid-state method. In accordance with the given chemical formula, stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (with 3&nbsp;wt% excess of sodium, 99%, Aladdin), Ca(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (99%, Aladdin), Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (with 3&nbsp;wt% excess of lithium, 99%, Aladdin), NiO (99%, Macklin), MnO<sub>2</sub> (99%, Macklin), and NaF (99%, Aladdin) were homogeneously mixed through ball milling at a rotation speed of 300&nbsp;rpm for 12&nbsp;h. Subsequently, the blended raw materials were dried at 120&nbsp;°C for 1&nbsp;h and then cold-pressed at a pressure of 10&nbsp;MPa for 5&nbsp;min, resulting in compacted pellets. Then, the pellets were subjected to a sintering process in the air at 900&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;h, employing a heating and cooling rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup>. After cooling to 150&nbsp;°C, the materials were finely ground into powder and transferred to an Ar-filled glove box for storage.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202407519-sec-0140-title"> Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>Powder XRD data were collected using a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.5406 Å). The XRD measurements spanned a 2<i>θ</i> range of 10°–80°. The obtained XRD patterns were subjected to Rietveld refinement analysis using the GSAS program.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0017_R_d4633268e1940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In situ XRD measurements were carried out using a custom-designed in situ battery holder, employing beryllium metal as the X-ray entrance window. The in situ XRD experiments were performed on a Bruker D8 Discover diffractometer equipped with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.5406 Å). The measurements were conducted at a fixed current density of 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. The unit cell parameter variations were quantified as the difference between the maximum and minimum values of the cell volume and the <i>c</i>-axis parameter divided by their respective initial values (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The Ni K-edge and Mn K-edge XAFS measurements were conducted at the ultrahigh-performance XAFS spectroscopy (Rapid XAFS) facility in transmission mode. The XAFS spectra were subsequently analyzed using the ATHENA software package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0033_R_d4633268e1955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Synchrotron Radiat.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The elemental stoichiometry in each sample was determined by Optima 2000-DV inductively coupled plasma emission spectrometry (PerkinElmer). The morphology of samples was analyzed utilizing scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Phenom Pharos G2), coupled with EDS for elemental characterization, employing an operating voltage of 15&nbsp;kV. Transmission electron microscopy specimens were prepared by focused ion/SEM dual beam microscope (Thermo Scientific Helios 5 UX). Subsequently, HRTEM images were acquired using Thermo Scientific Talos F200X G2 operated at 200&nbsp;kV. Geometric phase analysis was employed to analyze the stress distribution in the HRTEM images.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0034_R_d4633268e1961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Ultramicroscopy.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All SS NMR measurements were conducted on a 500&nbsp;MHz solid-state NMR spectrometer using a BRUKER 1.3&nbsp;mm triple resonance (H/X/Y) 500&nbsp;MHz CP-MAS WB DVT probe operating at a spinning rate of 55&nbsp;kHz. For the <sup>7</sup>Li NMR experiments, a recycle delay of 0.02 s and a 90° pulse length of 1.2 µs were employed. The <sup>7</sup>Li spectra were acquired using a stepwise spin-echo (90° pulse–<i>τ</i>–180° pulse–<i>τ</i>) pulse sequence and the irradiation frequency was incrementally adjusted with a step size of 0.18&nbsp;MHz. In the case of the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR experiments, the recycle delay and 90° pulse length were set to 0.02 s and 1.6 µs for the spin-echo pulse, respectively. Moreover, a pj-MATPASS pulse sequence was applied to obtain isotropic <sup>7</sup>Li NMR spectra. The chemical shifts of <sup>7</sup>Li and <sup>23</sup>Na were referenced to LiCl solution (0&nbsp;ppm) and NaCl (7.2&nbsp;ppm), respectively. The XPS analysis was performed using an ESCALAB Xi+ spectrometer, which was equipped with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray radiation source for excitation. Operando DEMS experiment was performed with a Swagelok-type cell and a commercial quadrupole mass spectrometer (Hiden Analytical). High purity Ar (99.999%) at a speed of 0.2&nbsp;mL min<sup>−1</sup> was used as the carrier gas and the galvanostatic charge–discharge during the DEMS test was performed on a LAND battery testing system with a current density of 80&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202407519-sec-0150-title"> Electrochemical Tests</h4>
                  
                  <p>The cathode slurry was formulated by blending the cathode active material (80&nbsp;wt%), acetylene black (10&nbsp;wt%), and polyvinylidene fluoride (10&nbsp;wt%) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone. The resulting slurry was then coated onto aluminum foil current collectors. In the half-cell assembly, the synthesized layered oxide material was employed as the cathode with a loading of 2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, sodium metal served as the anode, and a 1.0&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution dissolved in a mixed solvent of 100% propylene carbonate and 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (in volume) was used as the electrolyte. For the full-cell assembly, the prepared layered oxide material was utilized as the cathode with a loading of 4.2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, while hard carbon (Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd.) was employed as the anode with a loading of 1.9&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The electrolyte consisted of a 1.0&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution dissolved in a mixed solvent of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (EC:DEC = 50:50, vol%). To presodiate the cathode and overcome the irreversible capacity of hard carbon in the first cycle, the NCLNMOF electrode was assembled with sodium metal to form a half-cell, short-circuiting it for 10 s by connecting the cathode and the anode with a metal wire, and then removing the cathode. The hard carbon anode was assembled with sodium metal to form another half-cell and subjected to a one-cycle sodium preintercalation and deintercalation process between 0.005 and 2.5&nbsp;V at 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and then removing the hard carbon electrode. A glass fiber (GF/D Whatman) separator with a diameter of 19&nbsp;mm was employed, and the electrochemical performance of the samples was evaluated in 2016-type coin cells. Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were conducted using the Neware battery cycler (MIHW-200-160CH-B, Shenzhen, China). For galvanostatic intermittent titration technique measurements, the cells were cycled at a current density of 16&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, with each galvanostatic current applied for 5&nbsp;min followed by a rest period of 50&nbsp;min. The rate capability was evaluated by performing galvanostatic charge–discharge tests at current densities ranging from 16 to 1600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. All electrochemical tests were performed at 30&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407519-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202407519-sec-0160-title"> DFT Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The DFT calculations were performed on Vienna ab initio Simulation Package<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0035_R_d4633268e2028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Phys. Rev. B.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the exchange-correlation interactions of electron were described with spin-polarized GGA and parameterized by Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof formula.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407519-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407519-bib-0036_R_d4633268e2033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Phys. Rev. B.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The projector-augmented wave approach and GGA + <i>U</i> method were used to evaluate the electron–ion interactions and the localization of the d electrons of the TM ions, respectively. The <i>U</i> value of Mn and Ni set to 3.9 and 6.2&nbsp;eV and the wave functions were expended by plane wave with a kinetic energy cutoff of 520&nbsp;eV and a <i>k</i>-point mesh resolution of 3 × 3 × 2. For structural relaxations and self-consistent calculations, the convergence thresholds for total energy and force were 10<sup>−5</sup> eV and 0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. For the DFT calculations, a 2 × 2 × 1 supercell, which contained 16 Na, 48 O, 8 Ni, and 16 Mn was adopted. Meanwhile, the migration energy barriers of Ca<sup>2+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> were calculated using CI-NEB method with the same force and energy convergence.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
